  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_255eb7e8-51fd-4b95-a73b-1d948ceff815'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10508667036469388648">One of the best-loved of Nabokov’s novels, <i>Pnin</i> features his funniest and most heart-rending character. Professor Timofey Pnin is a haplessly disoriented Russian émigré precariously employed on an American college campus in the 1950's. Pnin struggles to maintain his dignity through a series of comic and sad misunderstandings, all the while falling victim both to subtle aca</span>
  <span id="freeText10508667036469388648" style="display:none">One of the best-loved of Nabokov’s novels, <i>Pnin</i> features his funniest and most heart-rending character. Professor Timofey Pnin is a haplessly disoriented Russian émigré precariously employed on an American college campus in the 1950's. Pnin struggles to maintain his dignity through a series of comic and sad misunderstandings, all the while falling victim both to subtle academic conspiracies and to the manipulations of a deliberately unreliable narrator.<br /><br />Initially an almost grotesquely comic figure, Pnin gradually grows in stature by contrast with those who laugh at him. Whether taking the wrong train to deliver a lecture in a language he has not mastered or throwing a faculty party during which he learns he is losing his job, the gently preposterous hero of this enchanting novel evokes the reader’s deepest protective instinct.<br /><br />Serialized in <i>The New Yorker</i> and published in book form in 1957, <i>Pnin</i> brought Nabokov both his first National Book Award nomination and hitherto unprecedented popularity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10508667036469388648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_65226271" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65226271" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65226271?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any serious student of literature &amp; language.  </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=%D1%80%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F">россия</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=author-author">author-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65226271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17284848396275302890"><b><i>“Some people—and I am one of them—hate happy ends. We feel cheated. Harm is the norm. Doom should not jam. The avalanche stopping in its tracks a few feet above the cowering village behaves not only unnaturally but unethically.”</i><br/>Pnin ~~ Vladimir Nabokov</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495686333i/22845695.png' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I have never read anything like Pnin ~~ Nabokov uses language like no other writer I've read before. I am riveted by both this book and Nabokov”s writing. <br/><br/>The strength of "Pnin" is its title character, Russian emigrate and professor, Timofey Pnin. </span>
  <span id="freeText17284848396275302890" style="display:none"><b><i>“Some people—and I am one of them—hate happy ends. We feel cheated. Harm is the norm. Doom should not jam. The avalanche stopping in its tracks a few feet above the cowering village behaves not only unnaturally but unethically.”</i><br>Pnin ~~ Vladimir Nabokov</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495686333i/22845695.png" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I have never read anything like Pnin ~~ Nabokov uses language like no other writer I've read before. I am riveted by both this book and Nabokov”s writing. <br><br>The strength of "Pnin" is its title character, Russian emigrate and professor, Timofey Pnin. A protagonist could hardly be more charming and lovable; Pnin's cultural and linguistic difficulties in adapting to America afford Nabokov plenty of opportunity for jokes and puns. The novel is astoundingly amusing, and the prose a sheer delight.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1571619318i/28326707.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="17284848396275302890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_71803710" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/71803710" />
    <a title="Geoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126266-geoff"><img alt="Geoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399505224p2/126266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/71803710?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff" href="/user/show/126266-geoff">Geoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126266-geoff?shelf=volodya">volodya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer71803710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7796955133404238622">If one wanted to undertake a neat little study of Nabokov’s fictional prowess, they should read <i>Lolita</i> and <i>Pnin</i> back to back. They were written concurrently, in little middle-American roadside motels (the ones that are chronicled so abundantly in <i>Lolita</i>) during Nabokov and Véra’s summer-long butterfly hunting tours. Pnin was Nabokov’s antidote and respite from Humbert’s grotesqueries, the opposite pole of character, and we should marvel at the achievement that while he was creating the most erud</span>
  <span id="freeText7796955133404238622" style="display:none">If one wanted to undertake a neat little study of Nabokov’s fictional prowess, they should read <i>Lolita</i> and <i>Pnin</i> back to back. They were written concurrently, in little middle-American roadside motels (the ones that are chronicled so abundantly in <i>Lolita</i>) during Nabokov and Véra’s summer-long butterfly hunting tours. Pnin was Nabokov’s antidote and respite from Humbert’s grotesqueries, the opposite pole of character, and we should marvel at the achievement that while he was creating the most erudite predator in the history of literature, he was at the same time moulding this Pnin from his most gentle clay, birthing his most sympathetic creature. The punning savagery of <i>Lolita</i> could not be farther away from Pnin’s sadly sweet sentimentality, and <i>Pnin</i> the book is the most touching Nabokov work I’ve encountered. Nabokov clearly loved this man, and while it is inevitable from page one that Humbert is a doomed, delirious soul, Pnin, whose doom seems always a hair’s width away, is almost kept from calamity by the reader’s sympathies for him alone- I challenge you to give this book a go and not get misty-eyed at Pnin giving water to a chirping squirrel (Pnin’s ever present squirrels; squirrel, from the Greek meaning “shadow-tail”; the “shade” behind Pnin’s heart; which Shade reminds one of that other novel where Pnin appears); Pnin ineptly attempting to extricate his automobile from a gravelly road; Pnin recollecting his beloved Misha under a sky stained red by sunset as he strolls among adumbral New England pines; Pnin dreaming his ghost father’s taking of a rook in a phantom chess match; Pnin breaking into hot tears at the cinematic depiction of a sun-struck Russian arbor; Pnin's defenestration of an unwanted soccer ball from a bedroom window; Pnin attempting to attain sleep through a backache as the wind ripples a puddle in the street, making of a telephone wire’s reflection the jagged angles of an ECG monitor; Pnin mustering quiet dignity and meticulously washing the dishes. Anyone acquainted with Nabokov’s biographical particularities can easily identify parallels between Pnin’s history and the author’s; but for Nabokov the private world was an impenetrable fortress, and any similarities that feed Pnin’s past should only be taken for what they are- inverse parallels, plays of imagination, refractions of a shared history that could be the story of many Russian expatriates who fled Fascism farther and farther west. Russia Abroad in the twentieth century is among the most fascinating literary diaspora, an inexhaustible well of insight into the limits of historical endurance. <i>Pnin</i> is a tenderly executed work by the man who continues to prove that he was the colossus of these wanderers- those who kept untouchable Russia alive and intact, at least in memory and imagination, wherever they might have been scattered.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7796955133404238622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1402984781" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1402984781" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1402984781?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32077113-pnin">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The other half</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Fionnuala by: </span>
      Half the reading world
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=nabokov-novels">nabokov-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=treasured-reading-experience">treasured-reading-experience</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=to-dip-into-again-and-again">to-dip-into-again-and-again</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1402984781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5102561340532552576"><b>The Revenge of Timofey Pnin</b><br/><br/>The traffic light was red. Timofey Pavlovich Pnin sat patiently at the steering wheel of his blue sedan directly behind a giant truck loaded with barrels of Budweiser, the inferior version of the Budvar he'd enjoyed in his Prague student days. On the passenger seat of the sedan, his paws resting on the open window, sat Gamlet, the stray dog Pnin had been feeding for the past few months, slowly encouraging the timid animal's trust. Gamlet had been unsure about the trip,</span>
  <span id="freeText5102561340532552576" style="display:none"><b>The Revenge of Timofey Pnin</b><br /><br />The traffic light was red. Timofey Pavlovich Pnin sat patiently at the steering wheel of his blue sedan directly behind a giant truck loaded with barrels of Budweiser, the inferior version of the Budvar he'd enjoyed in his Prague student days. On the passenger seat of the sedan, his paws resting on the open window, sat Gamlet, the stray dog Pnin had been feeding for the past few months, slowly encouraging the timid animal's trust. Gamlet had been unsure about the trip, reluctant to enter the car after Pnin had loaded the last boxes and suitcases and finally locked the door of the house he’d lived in for such a brief period. The dog ran around the yard in circles hesitating between going and staying until finally, much to Pnin's relief, he jumped on board.<br /><br />But now Gamlet was looking back in the direction they had come with increasing anxiety.<br /><br />Pnin glanced in the wing mirror. On the sidewalk, a man with a large and angry dog was hurrying towards them. The dog was straining at the leash, barking aggressively. Gamlet became more anxious and yapped madly in retaliation. Pnin recognised the dog immediately. It was Kykapeky’s dog, Kykapeky, the strutting director of the English Department, whose speciality was not Shakespeare or Milton or Wordsworth, but rather the impersonation of his unfortunate colleagues. Pnin knew himself to be the most unfortunate of the entire list. He had walked in on such impersonations many times, heard the sudden silence, seen people attempt to assume serious expressions. He'd felt the tension of modest guilt in the air, but noticed that some, like Kakadu from the French Department, didn’t even try to hide their sneers.<br /><br />But the man holding the dog was not Kykapeky. <br /><br />No, not Kykapeky, and not Kakadu either. <br /><br />It was Kukushka!<br /><br />Pnin had hoped to be well clear of Waindell University before his old rival arrived to take over the Russian Department, a department that Pnin had built by himself from nearly nothing. Pnin didn't suppose the man had changed much. He would be the same old Kukushka, taking, always taking, leaving nothing but discards. <br /><br />And now Kukushka would take Gamlet too. The dog would surely jump out of the car window. When he did, Pnin would not stop to retrieve him. No, he would leave Gamlet on the sidewalk, leave him to Kukushka just as he’d surrendered many beloved things to that man in the past.<br /><br />At that very moment the lights changed and the dog hesitated and Pnin accelerated as soon as the truck moved off, and he was away, striking west as so many times before. But this time, he was heading towards real freedom. As the blue sedan picked up speed, the dog stopped barking and lay down on the passenger seat and Pnin allowed himself to relax. He had escaped Kukushka finally and forever, leaving him to rot, alongside Kykapeky and Kakadu and the rest of the ptitsa, in the brackish backwaters of the miserable university town of Waindellville. <br /><br />................................................<br /><br />Index of Russian words used in this piece:<br />Gamlet = Hamlet, the prince of hesitation, and Pnin's favourite play.<br />Kykapeky = the sound a cockerel makes in Russian. The Head of the English Department in Waindell was called Jack Cockerell.<br />Kakadu = cockatoo. Kaka sounds like 'caca' which means 'shit' in French making the word particularly fitting for Blorenge, the Head of the French Department, who could barely speak French and thought Chateaubriand was a famous chef.<br />Kukushka = cuckoo, the robber bird, used here to stand in for the new Head of the Russian Department who had ousted Pnin in Waindellville as he had ousted him in Russia long years before.<br />Ptitsa = fowl, as in barnyard fowl<br />None of these names appear in Nabokov's novel - I've simply imagined what the very observant Pnin might have called his unpleasant colleagues, and his beloved dog, in the safety of his own mind.<br /><br />................................................<br />Edit: October 6th<br />Pnin was my first Nabokov. I'm now reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7805.Pale_Fire" title="Pale Fire by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">Pale Fire</a> and I'm glad to see Pnin turning up on page 221 wearing a Hawaiian shirt. So he did go west!<br />And there's an index of foreign words at the end of Pale Fire, and lots of references to birds...<br /><br />Edit: October 9th<br />I'm now reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/71552.The_Real_Life_of_Sebastian_Knight" title="The Real Life of Sebastian Knight by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">The Real Life of Sebastian Knight</a> and on page 62, there's a reference to a possible book title, 'Cock Robin Hits Back', which along with the ornithological parallel echoes 'The Revenge of Timofey Pnin' a little...<br /><br />Edit: November 25th<br />In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8147.The_Gift" title="The Gift by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gift</a>, the narrator mentions a review writer (he calls him a 'critique-bouffe') who liked to... <i>provide the book with his own ending</i>...</span>
  <a data-text-id="5102561340532552576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2152785956" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2152785956" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2152785956?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=russia-ukraine">russia-ukraine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2152785956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2181867755102102494">Whilst a certain novel featuring a middle-aged man infatuating over his seduction of a 12-year-old girl was causing a storm in the literary world, along came the gentle breeze that was Pnin. Another remarkable character in a career littered with remarkable characters. After arriving in America in 1940, with wife Véra, and son Dmitri, as virtually broke refugees from Nazi-occupied France, Nabokov was able to find employment as a university teacher of Russian and comparative literature, first at i</span>
  <span id="freeText2181867755102102494" style="display:none">Whilst a certain novel featuring a middle-aged man infatuating over his seduction of a 12-year-old girl was causing a storm in the literary world, along came the gentle breeze that was Pnin. Another remarkable character in a career littered with remarkable characters. After arriving in America in 1940, with wife Véra, and son Dmitri, as virtually broke refugees from Nazi-occupied France, Nabokov was able to find employment as a university teacher of Russian and comparative literature, first at in Massachusetts, then Cornell University in upstate New York. This clearly influenced Pnin. From an early stage in the development of the character of Pnin, Nabokov planned to write a series of stories about about the comical misadventures of an expatriate Russian professor on his way to deliver a lecture to a women's club in a small American town, which could be published independently in the New Yorker, which later was strung together to make a seriously good book. This proved to be a shrewd professional strategy. It also partly explains the unusual form of Pnin and how best to describe it. A short novel? a collection of short stories of set pieces? anyway, Nabokov poignantly sets about tracing Timofey Pnin's quest, which is ultimately frustrated, to find a home, or to make himself at home in the alien small town of Waindell.<br /><br />Taking the small world pastoral campus setting, and removing the hustle and bustle of modern urban life, Pnin contains the fictional elements of different subgenres, but ultimately, this is quintessentially true Nabokovian territory, which goes about having a family resemblance to his other works without being exactly like any of them. For those who know their Nabokov well, it is full of allusions to and foreshadowings of those other works, especially Pale Fire (my personal favourite) where Pnin reappears, happily ensconced in a tenured professorship at Wordsmith College. Nabokov does not aim simply at a perfect match between his language and his imagined world. There are always strong reminders in his work where reality is larger, denser, and full of everyday occurrences encompassing his vision. Moments when the discourse suddenly seems to take off on its own and break through the formal limits of the story into the world outside the story, where the author and reader coexist.<br /><br />Pnin himself is lots of fun to read about, even if he struggles to understand American humor, making this one of Nabokov's most joyous reads, he is particularly sensitive to noise and always hopes that the next house he moves to will be free of this nuisance. He is charming in his rambling ways and lectures, but cannot deliver a prepared speech without burying his head in the text and reading in a soporific monotone. He is obsessively careful, but still manages to get himself into awful jams. It's a character just so easy to fall in with. Lolita will always be the novel for which Nabokov will be best known, it went on to sell millions worldwide, and completely eclipsed Pnin in the public consciousness, but reading this again for the third time, just goes to set in stone Nabokov's very high standards, and a status of being one of the top novelists of the 20th century.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2181867755102102494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1003607981" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003607981" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1003607981?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=faves">faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1003607981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16123098396977869849">I recently read Doctor Zhivago which Nabokov hated. You could say these two books are the antithesis of each other. Zhivago strives to depict a poetic vision of real life on a huge canvas and find meaning therein; Pnin is self-pleasuring art for art’s sake on a tiny canvas. Nabokov isn’t remotely interested in “real life” or deep meaning or huge canvases. He passes over the Russian Revolution in a couple of sentences whereas a description of a room that will only feature once in the entire novel</span>
  <span id="freeText16123098396977869849" style="display:none">I recently read Doctor Zhivago which Nabokov hated. You could say these two books are the antithesis of each other. Zhivago strives to depict a poetic vision of real life on a huge canvas and find meaning therein; Pnin is self-pleasuring art for art’s sake on a tiny canvas. Nabokov isn’t remotely interested in “real life” or deep meaning or huge canvases. He passes over the Russian Revolution in a couple of sentences whereas a description of a room that will only feature once in the entire novel is likely to receive an entire long paragraph. Wisdom doesn’t interest him much either except as a reliable source of caustic mockery. Psychotherapy is one of his targets in Pnin. Just as he mocks a lot of the devices favoured by novelists. There are two instances in this novel of Nabokov cleverly creating a great deal of sympathy for Pnin and in both he takes away our sympathy as soon as he’s got it. These involve Pnin catching the wrong train to an important lecture he’s due to give (he makes it there on time regardless) and of Pnin receiving a cherished bowl from his son which he believes he has destroyed when he lets slip a pair of nutcrackers into the soapy washing up water (turns out to be a worthless glass he’s broken). Pnin is constantly being misled by subjective interpretations of objective reality but it doesn’t really matter, it doesn’t do him any real harm. There’s a sense Nabokov thinks of everything as a storm in a teacup, even the Russian revolution and Hitler’s war, from both of which Pnin emerges unscathed as if they’re of little more importance than a thunderstorm. If you’re God there’s a lot of truth in this point of view and Nabokov can come across as believing himself to be a deity of sorts. <br /><br />I’ve just read some of the negative reviews of this and the word “boring” crops up a lot. And depending on the page you’re on Pnin is either brilliant or, as these people say, can be a bit boring. That is to say it’s boring if you’re not a great fan of elaborate description of furniture, landscape or physiognomy. There is a lot of wordsmithery spent on ephemera. In fact I don’t think I’ve ever read a novel that so swiftly and frequently transited me from joy to boredom. There’s one of the best comic scenes in literature involving the hapless Russian professor, a squirrel and a water fountain. It’s comic genius but on anything but a superficial level it’s also meaningless, like one of those cute animal YouTube videos. That one scene maybe sums up this novel better than any review could – the slightly hollow interior behind the brilliant surface. <br /><br />All in all Pnin is a pale understudy to Pale Fire in which he finds a dazzling form to poke fun at his targets here, exile into a foreign culture and academia. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16123098396977869849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2859016503033816733">485.	Pnin, Vladimir Nabokov<br/><br/>Pnin is Vladimir Nabokov's 13th novel and his fourth written in English; it was published in 1957. Pnin features his funniest and most heart-rending character. <br/><br/>Professor Timofey Pnin is a haplessly disoriented Russian émigré precariously employed on an American college campus in the 1950's. <br/><br/>Pnin struggles to maintain his dignity through a series of comic and sad misunderstandings, all the while falling victim both to subtle academic conspiracies and to the manipulation</span>
  <span id="freeText2859016503033816733" style="display:none">485.	Pnin, Vladimir Nabokov<br /><br />Pnin is Vladimir Nabokov's 13th novel and his fourth written in English; it was published in 1957. Pnin features his funniest and most heart-rending character. <br /><br />Professor Timofey Pnin is a haplessly disoriented Russian émigré precariously employed on an American college campus in the 1950's. <br /><br />Pnin struggles to maintain his dignity through a series of comic and sad misunderstandings, all the while falling victim both to subtle academic conspiracies and to the manipulations of a deliberately unreliable narrator.<br /><br />پنین - ولادیمیر ناباکوف (شوقستان، کارنامه 2005) ادبیات؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش ماه سپتامبر سال 2005میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: پنین؛ نویسنده: ولادیمیر ناباکف؛ مترجم: بهمن خسروی؛ ویراستار بهناز بهادری فر؛ تهران، نسل شوقستان؛ 1382؛ در 271ص؛ شابک 9649346430؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان روس تبار امریکایی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: پنین؛ نویسنده: ولادیمیر ناباکف؛ مترجم: رضا رضایی؛ تهران، کارنامه، 1383؛ در 276ص؛ مصور؛ شابک 9644310470؛ نمایه دارد؛ چاپ دیگر 1393؛ در 302ص؛ <br /><br />در همان آغاز رمان، شاید اگر آن دره ی بزرگوار بین زبان روسی و انگلیسی وجود نداشت، آن بلا در سفر با اتوبوس، بر سر «پنین» نمیآمد؛ «تیموفی پنین»، شیفته ی زبان روسی است، و با چنان ستایشی واژه های زبان روسی را ادا میکند، که گویی در حال خواندن اپرایی از موتزارت (موتسارت) است، اما این توان و آشنایی «پنین» بر زبان روسی، و شیفتگی ایشان به این زبان، همان چیزهایی ست که موضوع خنده، و مضحکه ی شاگردان انگلیسی زبان او هستند؛ این واکنشهای تند «ناباکوف»، به پیرامون خویش، به نوعی نتیجه ی همین سرگشتگی، بین سه گروه در ینگه دنیا است، و «ناباکوف» در بسیاری از مواقع، از این حمله ها، به عنوان سپر آفندی، سود میبرد؛ بهترین راه رخنه به جهان پیچیده ی «ناباکوف»، رمانهای ایشان هستند، و در اینراه باید این جمله ی «ناباکوف» را، هماره در پس ذهن داشت، که: «پنین خود من است.»؛ <br /><br />در رمان «پنین»، «ناباکوف» با واژه های خویش، به سه گروه، یورش میبرد، و میتازد<br /><br />گروه نخست: انقلابیهای شوروی، و کسانیکه در کشور شوراها قدرت را در دست دارند؛<br /><br />گروه دوم: روسهای ساکن آمریکا، آن دسته از روسهای بی بخاری، که مخالف انقلاب، و شیفته ی آمریکا هستند، و منتظرند شوروی از بین برود، تا با عزت و افتخار، به روسیه ی خویش برگردند؛<br /><br />و سومین گروه: آمریکائیهائی که، هر چند استاد دانشگاه هستند، و از فرهیختگان جامعه به شمار میروند، از دیدگاه «ناباکوف»، در بلاهت و سطحی نگری، چیزی کمتر از دو گروه پیشین ندارند؛<br /><br />در این میان، قربانی اصلی خود «پنین» است؛ مردیکه در بین این سه دسته گرفتار شده، و راهی ندارد جز آنکه در جنگل آمریکا، به مرز جنون برسد؛ مسئله زبان در رمان «پنین»، خود نیاز به نگارشی پر دامنه دارد<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 26/04/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1275591925" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1275591925" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1275591925?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13558837-pnin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=other-awards-w">other-awards-w</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=for_legacy">for_legacy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1275591925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12051645115912209995">The evening lessons were always the most difficult. Drained of ambulating the willing grey cells throughout the carnage of day classes, the young readers, almost resignedly, filled the quiet room at the end of the corridor. A subdued tête-à-tête, almost at once, broke into a charlatan laughter and the very next moment, died in their bosoms as Professor Pnin entered the classroom. <br/><br/>Straightening the meagre crop on his head and adjusting (and re-adjusting) his tortoise-shell glasses, he cleared his</span>
  <span id="freeText12051645115912209995" style="display:none">The evening lessons were always the most difficult. Drained of ambulating the willing grey cells throughout the carnage of day classes, the young readers, almost resignedly, filled the quiet room at the end of the corridor. A subdued tête-à-tête, almost at once, broke into a charlatan laughter and the very next moment, died in their bosoms as Professor Pnin entered the classroom. <br /><br />Straightening the meagre crop on his head and adjusting (and re-adjusting) his tortoise-shell glasses, he cleared his throat.<br /><br />Pnin: Good Evening.<br /><br />Class: Good Evening, Professor.<br /><br />Pnin <i>(cheerily)</i>: I am glad to see the attendance has brimmed to full today. <i>[Pause]</i> Alright then. Would all of you open your notes now? We shall take each one of your observations on Turgenev’s prose and discuss threadbare their meaning and implications on the Russian Literature fabric.<br /><br /><i>[Silence]</i><br /><br />Pnin: Ladies and Gentlemen, please open your notes.<br /><br /><i>[Silence]</i><br /><br />Pnin <i>(in a mildly concerned tone)</i>: What is the matter? I can see your notes sitting pretty on your tables and yet you do not touch them? May I please be privy to your thoughts?<br /><br />Josephine: Professor, we do have notes but they do not concern Turgenev’s prose.<br /><br />Pnin: What do they concern then?<br /><br />Josephine: You.<br /><br />Pnin: Me?<br /><br />Charles: Indeed Professor.<br /><br />Pnin: But why?<br /><br />Charles: Because that’s what is the homework we got – to analyse <i>your</i> publication on Turgenev’s prose, “Fathers and Sons – A Literary Bond”.<br /><br />Pnin: No, no! I wanted you to read “Fathers and Sons” by Turgenev for analysis!<br /><br />Eileen: Professor, you have given us the name of the wrong book then. Or perhaps we misunderstood your intentions. <i>Again</i>.<br /><br />Pnin: What? But how is this…… <i>(and his voice took a u-turn and trudged inside his mouth and jagged right into his head.)</i><br /><br />Eileen <i>(excitedly)</i>: But we have made some fascinating observations about you, Professor! You may like to hear them!<br /><br />With the opportunity to assess the literary quotient of his class vanished like the hair on his head, he settled for the less worthy evaluation of their intelligence quotient.<br /><br />Pnin <i>(reluctantly)</i>: Very well then. You may show me the mirror, Miss Eileen.<br /><br />Eileen: Actually, you began with the mission of dissecting Pushkin’s oeuvre but never got the book since you yourself had blocked it from issuing it to anyone else! I mean Professor Pnin had Pushkin allotted to himself in the system which he never got and could neither reallot it to Professor Pnin since it was always out of library!<br /><br />Pnin: Yeees. It was an obscene revenge of the computer against my disdain for it.<br /><br />Eileen <i>(supressing laughter)</i>: And it happened often! But the university still kept you since <b>
  <i>it was fashionable to have atleast one distinguished fr*** on the staff.</i>
</b><br /><br />Pnin: Fr*** ??<br /><br />Josephine: Leave that, Professor! See, what I have found! Even your prodigal son, Victor, who delved in scholastic art from a tender age of four, could not decorate your limping English. Your reference to a noisy neighborhood as <b>
  <i>sonic disturbance</i>
</b>, house-warming party as <b>
  <i>house-heating party</i>
</b>, could pass, at best, as puerile. <i>
  <b>If your Russian was music, your English was murder!</b>
</i><br /><br />Pnin: Why should I be a custodian of English when I know that Russian is a far superior language?<br /><br />Charles: Perhaps because the former is more widely spoken?<br /><br />Pnin: Ah, yes. <i>(cheekily)</i> My wife was good at it.<br /><br />Charles: <i>(competing cheekily)</i> A little too good, may I add, Professor. She affirmed her proficiency by alluding an American Psychoanalyst in its lucid fold.<br /><br />Pnin: Mr. Charles, you may refrain from making personal remarks.<br /><br />Charles: Its <i>YOUR</i> publication we are taking about, Professor!<br /><br />Pnin: I know, I know. Miss Josephine, do you have any more value additions?<br /><br />Josephine: You went to great length to spread the sumptuous roots of Russian Literature; why, you took to Cremona on a wrong train! But your passionate erudition got you patient listeners and appreciative academicians. <br /><br />Pnin: Thank you, Miss Josephine.<br /><br />Josephine: You were also a strong and loving father to Victor as both of you, in abundance, were each other’s reflection – non-confirmists, impulsive, passionate and unrecognized scholars.<br /><br />Pnin: Yes, I tried to be Victor’s shadow. He liked me, I think. Because I understood him. His artistic ebullience needed channelling into the right skies and I attempted to hold him aloft when he started stepping up. <br /><br />Eileen: But you lost your link with Russian Literature, its prospective followers and your dear ones owing to your diminutive circle, subservient approach, vanilla judgement and ill-placed magnanimity.<br /><br />Pnin <i>(pensively)</i>: Yes, I have. But I haven’t lost my link with life. Yes, I have abandoned many parts of me; rather many parts of me have abandoned me like an ugly aberrant. But I believe there was some purpose in all of it. The purpose got clearer as the power of my spectacles increased; ironic as it may sound. Life is still like a long, beautiful Pushkin’s poem which I can read, once again, from the beginning and find new meaning in it. And if I ever struggle, I will have you good Samaritans to adjust my antennae. <br /><br />Class <i>(in unison)</i>: Yes Professor.<br /><br />Pnin: Alright then. I thank you for spending precious time out and understanding my life..…<br /><br />Charles <i>(curtly)</i>: It was a homework, Professor.<br /><br />Pnin: Ah yes. My apologies. Well, I will see you in three days then. Good night.<br /><br />Class: Goodnight Professor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12051645115912209995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1944710114" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1944710114" />
    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1944710114?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307787477" href="/book/show/10455623-pnin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=2017-book-challenge">2017-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1944710114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4020032824588812205">I would call this 1957 Nabokov novel a tragicomedy, leaning more to the comedy. Timofey Pnin is a likeable Russian emigre, a nice man, maybe too nice for his own good. Pnin is an assistant professor at fictional Wainsdell College, probably modeled after Cornell University where Nabokov taught. Even though Pnin has become an American citizen, he still struggles with the English language. He has difficultly being understood by his students and his colleagues. He makes his way through life in an ho</span>
  <span id="freeText4020032824588812205" style="display:none">I would call this 1957 Nabokov novel a tragicomedy, leaning more to the comedy. Timofey Pnin is a likeable Russian emigre, a nice man, maybe too nice for his own good. Pnin is an assistant professor at fictional Wainsdell College, probably modeled after Cornell University where Nabokov taught. Even though Pnin has become an American citizen, he still struggles with the English language. He has difficultly being understood by his students and his colleagues. He makes his way through life in an honest and but prideful manner, but things never turn out quite the way Timofey would like them too. I imagine most of the academics and professors who read this novel see a little of themselves in Timofey Pnin, or at least in someone they know.<br /><br />Wonderful character, excellent writing. 4 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="4020032824588812205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1403807968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1403807968?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32077113-pnin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1403807968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6299449694622783153"><br/>I had a professor, in fact he had no professor’s title, but we always addressed him that way. So, I had a professor who taught me maths. No, actually he was trying to teach me, he was doing his best to familiarize me with secrets of the queen of science. Alas ! I truly felt pity for him since I was stupendously immune to that knowledge. I was standing at the blackboard attempting to solve some mysterious to me equation and professor, waving his hand, would sigh then <i> get out of my sight, please </i></span>
  <span id="freeText6299449694622783153" style="display:none"><br />I had a professor, in fact he had no professor’s title, but we always addressed him that way. So, I had a professor who taught me maths. No, actually he was trying to teach me, he was doing his best to familiarize me with secrets of the queen of science. Alas ! I truly felt pity for him since I was stupendously immune to that knowledge. I was standing at the blackboard attempting to solve some mysterious to me equation and professor, waving his hand, would sigh then <i> get out of my sight, please </i>. Even today this recollection brings smile to my face. He was extraordinary teacher, demanding when it needed and lenient when he knew that his efforts after all would go down the drain. Fortunately for me he was not a type of crusader and knew which battles were lost before even started.<br /><br />He used to accompany us to many school outing and I had opportunity to know him also from more private side. I remember, it was shortly after the shooting of John Lennon and we wanted somehow commemorate him, and professor then submitted the plan to plant the trees. So we went to the forest district and planted them. Lennon’s oaks. Or our wintry foray to the mountains and New Year’s Eve spent in the snowbound tiny church where brethren offered to us hot tea. It tasted exquisitely in that cold night. <br /><br />He was charming man with great sense of humour. But there was about him, when I come to think about it now, some air of sadness and melancholia. I see him entering the class and throwing a register on his desk to stand at the window without a word for several minutes, sometimes even whole lesson. He came across as someone absent-minded and nonchalant. And a bit careless about his clothes in contrast to our other teacher who was very pedantic and used to wear his socks always under the colour of his shirts ( oh dear, these pink socks !). Oh, happy days.<br /><br />I’m not sure where this rambling and digressive writing is leading me since I was going to write about Pnin and <i> Pnin </i>. But entering pninian universe triggered this stupid device called memory and I bogged down in own recollections. But I've got to say for myself that Pnin himself said <i> you also will recollect the past with interest when old </i> .</span>
  <a data-text-id="6299449694622783153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1338506011" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1338506011" />
    <a title="Ritwik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24048474-ritwik"><img alt="Ritwik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461434494p2/24048474.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1338506011?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ritwik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ritwik" href="/user/show/24048474-ritwik">Ritwik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141183756" href="/book/show/134000.Pnin">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ritwik by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1338506011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10461009369416847295"><br/> Coming from the master word-smith, a critic and the dictator of the reading choices of legions of readers comes a book backed by a blurb which compares Nobokov to a standard stand-up comedian with a professional capacity of making the audience laugh hysterically. Sad to say, the humour in the books failed to appeal me and was eclipsed by the unfortunate tribulations that influenced the demure and naive professor Timofey Pnin's reputation amongst his associates and the staff of the University.<br/> T</span>
  <span id="freeText10461009369416847295" style="display:none"><br /> Coming from the master word-smith, a critic and the dictator of the reading choices of legions of readers comes a book backed by a blurb which compares Nobokov to a standard stand-up comedian with a professional capacity of making the audience laugh hysterically. Sad to say, the humour in the books failed to appeal me and was eclipsed by the unfortunate tribulations that influenced the demure and naive professor Timofey Pnin's reputation amongst his associates and the staff of the University.<br /> The book starts with Pnin, an emigre(immigrant) Russian professor struggling with English, sitting in the wrong train while he is already late for his lecture and loses his luggage. He is constantly made fun of and is often undermined by his superiors and colleagues. The humour revolves around such events affecting Pnin. Although frivolous by nature, Nabokov's character and the events bring out sympathy out of me as a reader which overlaps the humour quotient in the book.It might preliminarily seem like Nabokov furtively describing his experiences through the character of Pnin but makes brief appearances himself directly addressing the reader and reflecting on tender topics along life and death in beauteous prose maintained constantly throughout the book-<br /> <br /><i>I do not know if it ever has been noted before that one of the main characteristics of life is discreteness. Unless a film of flesh envelops us, we die. Man exists so far as he is separated from his surroundings. The cranium is a space traveller's helmet. Stay inside or you perish. Death is divestment, death is communion. It may be wonderful to mix with the landscape,but to do so is the end of tender ego.</i><br /> <br /> As a Russian literature enthusiast it is heartening to see the names of Turgenev, Pushkin, Gogol, Chekhov, Tolstoy, Lermontov flashing through now and then and I regret not reading 'Anna Karenina' before reading this to completely comprehend Professor Pnin's unbounded admiration for the book reasoned by his concept described as 'relativity of literature' in 'Anna Karenina'. Nabokov makes subtle references to other such great works and takes pot shots at Dostoevsky whom he criticized. There is an episode in which Pnin's step son Victor talks about books<br /><br /> <i>'Last summer I read Crime and ----' and a young yawn distended his staunchly smiling mouth.</i><br /><br /> The author vents his judgements and his reflections on books via the character of Pnin. It seems Pnin is Nabokov himself but he himself makes an appearance as an acquaintance of Pnin in the book.<br /><br /><br /> Nabokov relies heavily on his prose style and is dependant on his verbal contortions where his characters live in a world revolving around objects with an harmonical wholeness which only Nabokov could have conjured with his masterful prose. His gleaming literary insights as shown in his pedagogical approach confounded my bleak understanding of the study of literature as a subject but that seems to be deliberate from the writer's side. It takes time to acclimatize to his writing pattern and the plot might seem stale but his playfulness shines through as he smoothly transitions through multiple digressions and ends in a cyclic fashion which is impressive in itself.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10461009369416847295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15657895288902297804">Later Nabokov, oddly sweet compared with the more tart early novels. The wonder of putting trainers (Sneakers in certain jurisdictions) in the washing machine and listening to them running round or being taken as some kind of saint or angel as he sits broad smiling with a large Greek cross on his bare ches</span>
  <span id="freeText15657895288902297804" style="display:none">Later Nabokov, oddly sweet compared with the more tart early novels. Bad poetry is savaged only once.<br /><br />The eponymous Pnin, an ageing expatriate academic engaged in teaching Russian in small town America, is the hero of this oddly optimistic and even joyful novel. The wonder of putting trainers (Sneakers in certain jurisdictions) in the washing machine and listening to them running round or being taken as some kind of saint or angel as he sits broad smiling with a large Greek cross on his bare chest under a sunlamp outweigh the exile and failed marriage. Perhaps an alternative self portrait of the Author as a gangly optimist?</span>

  <span id="freeText744111519162168750" style="display:none">“Now a secret must be imparted. Professor Pnin was on the wrong train. He was unaware of it, and so was the conductor, already threading his way through the train to Pnin’s coach. As a matter of fact, Pnin at the moment felt very well satisfied with himself.”<br />Pnin is a stranger in a strange land – a learnt misfit in search of his singular niche, Don Quixote trying to win over an especially malicious windmill.<br />“‘Yes,’ said Pnin with a sigh, ‘intrigue is horrible, horrible. But, on the other side, honest work will always prove its advantage. You and I will give next year some splendid new courses which I have planned long ago. On Tyranny. On the Boot. On Nicholas the First. On all the precursors of modern atrocity. Hagen, when we speak of injustice, we forget Armenian massacres, tortures which Tibet invented, colonists in Africa.... The history of man is the history of pain!’”<br />Is Pnin really a stranger or is he the only true human being among all those intriguing academicians?</span>

<div id="review_3522774272" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3522774272" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3522774272?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/8971913-pnin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3522774272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15220203140099916910">Several short stories about an absent minded professor rammed together and called a novel (but that’s okay, people do it all the time), <i>Pnin</i> is almost beloved by readers who aren’t me. Professor Pnin with his hilariously broken English is allegedly endearing but I was not even slightly endeared. This was footling stuff. He gets on the wrong train. He nearly misses giving an evening lecture. He buys a football for a kid who doesn’t like football. He doesn’t realise his job is on the line. He talk</span>
  <span id="freeText15220203140099916910" style="display:none">Several short stories about an absent minded professor rammed together and called a novel (but that’s okay, people do it all the time), <i>Pnin</i> is almost beloved by readers who aren’t me. Professor Pnin with his hilariously broken English is allegedly endearing but I was not even slightly endeared. This was footling stuff. He gets on the wrong train. He nearly misses giving an evening lecture. He buys a football for a kid who doesn’t like football. He doesn’t realise his job is on the line. He talks to people. Some of them like him. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598813091i/30037487.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So that’s how <i>Pnin</i> sank down to the two star basement. But of course Vlad the Impaler is one of the all time English language stylists, so he could be writing about - oh, say, the experience of getting all your teeth pulled out – and it would be heavenly :<br><br><i>It surprised him to realize how fond he had been of his teeth. His tongue, a fat sleek seal, used to flop and slide so happily among the familiar rocks, checking the contours of a battered but still secure kingdom, plunging from cave to cove, climbing this jag, nuzzling that notch, finding a shred of sweet seaweed in the same old cleft but now not a landmark remained, and all there existed was a great dark wound, a terra incognita of gums which dread and disgust forbade one to investigate.</i><br><br>But there is not enough of this grotesquerie and way too much description of rooms. My God, writers love to describe rooms! Describing rooms must be as good as sex for writers. <br><br>VN wrote <i>Pnin</i> while he was stuck toward the end of writing <i>Lolita</i>, as a kind of holiday from the horror, which is like if the Beatles stopped recording "A Day in the Life" to knock off "Ob-La-Di Ob-La-Da", and if the melancholy uncomedy of <i>Pnin</i> gave VN the break he needed to complete his masterpiece, then I celebrate this very thin novel for that reason alone. <br><br>2.5 stars, rounded up to 3 to make me not look like an idiot - this is Nabokov!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15220203140099916910" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1284893086" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1284893086" />
    <a title="Stephen P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p"><img alt="Stephen P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461732018p2/11602286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1284893086?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen P" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p">Stephen P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13558837-pnin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11602286-stephen-p?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284893086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1653252097154494411">If in these beginning pages Nabokov is laying out how to read this work I can only smile, which I have been doing unnoticed since I opened the covers, and conclude that beneath the voice of erudition lies the eye wink of humor, underlined by the cunning of acerbic wit. All of this, each line will contribute to the meaning of the narrative, while the narrative itself will be a major event. <br/><br/>I shouldn’t forget, even though I don’t know what it means at this point, but I am reading it aloud to mysel</span>
  <span id="freeText1653252097154494411" style="display:none">If in these beginning pages Nabokov is laying out how to read this work I can only smile, which I have been doing unnoticed since I opened the covers, and conclude that beneath the voice of erudition lies the eye wink of humor, underlined by the cunning of acerbic wit. All of this, each line will contribute to the meaning of the narrative, while the narrative itself will be a major event. <br /><br />I shouldn’t forget, even though I don’t know what it means at this point, but I am reading it aloud to myself. Not out loud but in my mind I speak the words and again not realizing, am moving my lips around the sounds of the words.<br /><br />It begins with Pnin on a coach car of a train by himself. We learn he is on the wrong train. There is only one train for him. Will he find it? <br /><br />The camera fades into his life as a Professor of Russian at a small college. In the professor’s Russian class is but a handful of young students. Wanting to add humor he takes out an old Russian book leafing through it for some time to build up the tension that will lead to the guffaws as it did and does for him. As he reads he laughs till tears run down his cheeks. His students who do not have the knowledge of Russian vernacular to get this aging and literary-aligned humor, each in their own style are laughing at him laughing. A contagion. An airborne virus. He is clearly on the wrong…<br /><br />He and I are back on the wrong train. I may just stay on the wrong train. I never thought how much fun that may be. All the unexpected adventures. Living ones life on the wrong train can be a vital life or… a life isolated and sad. An early sign, this scene of wit yet excruciating pain for the reader, shows that Pnin has been extricated from his life, extricated from time, extricated from himself, in the aloneness of exile. The first shadow is cast though not spreading across the surface of buoyancy . <br /><br />Interesting, he is described as completely bald tan and vigorous looking. Even as he ages he maintains some of this. There was for me a vast dissonance between this and my internal image of him as a frail old man. An intricacy barely noticed in its passages.<br /><br />So, now I will turn the page to the actual seventh page of the text. Whew. Who knew? At 185 pages it will take weeks to finish. Weeks of pleasure. Returning to each paragraph read not for analysis but the sheer ephemeral floating amongst the rising words and bliss of cherished sound. <br /><br />It is never a matter of fighting to remain within the narrative. It flows on in its gossamer weave. Each word though calls for an admiration of its centrally carved space cradling the lone thread of letters sculpted for its fit. The carved space awaits its find for the sonority and the finely honed Nabokovian distinctions creating crystal lines outlining what only can be.<br /><br />Pnin is a man who lives alone but is not lonely. Renting a room in a house he moves frequently around the area of Waindell and its University. His classes are few and ill attended. He doesn’t care. His life of order is structured around his love of scholarship. Slipping into the library following his teaching he quickly vanishes into his research for his project he has been working on for years. He does not avoid reality but chooses to reside within the reality which he knows and understands, has sentimental and nostalgic feelings as well. This is a profound exploration of reality, identity, without the profundity. Rather it is woven within the seamless prose. Did I see an eye’s-wink? A wry smile half hidden? I love him being here with me and my living within the prose he offers. It is hard for me to imagine with the news blaring catastrophe nightly that ours is a good world but here for 185 pages it is. It is moments like these when I can’t believe how lucky I am. There are books! There are masters of craft! Judging by my shelves, more than I will be able to read in my lifetime. <br /><br />Even this though is a segue-way into the past. This is what exists for him. The traditions and conventions to join the dots needed for comfort or to who and what he is? Joining other Russian expats means listening to and be encouraged in assimilating into this new culture. What hails around him is a world which holds no interest. The present and future waver in the gray air with no beckoned finger or lured wink of an eye.<br /><br />The narrator is under no such constraints and in third person omniscient, we view through such magnificent set pieces that I wanted to place each in its own cushioned box and store beneath the sealed glass of a jeweled display. But then this narrator at brief instances switches into first person without a shimmer in the reader’s eye. Then at the end a masterful transition where in first person present, the narrator introduces us to the young Pnin. We discover him as a rose cheeked youth, an excellent student, vital, growing up. It is disconcerting in a lovely way. This sad man we have known for so long was once a youth? Smart and open eyed? But how…<br /><br />Ah. The revolution in Russia. The war. Displacement from all he knows. Can he continue to hold back time without suffering the abrasions of red slitted wounds? The final passage is a perfect example of Nabokovian prose and a wondrous end to this beloved book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1653252097154494411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434433202" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434433202?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2020">2020</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=american">american</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434433202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer955269524420314961"><i>“Why not leave their private sorrows to people? Is sorrow not, one asks, the only thing in the world people really possess?”</i> <br/>― Vladimir Nabokov, Pnin<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578889760i/28765935._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='500' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This isn't just the last nail in my Nabokov coffin, this is the ground thrown on the coffin. Finito sweet benito. I've now read all his ficiton (both those written in Russian and translated into English later and those written in English). It is kinda sad. But so too is Pnin. I'd call the novel melancholy, but it isn't quite sad or melancholy. There</span>
  <span id="freeText955269524420314961" style="display:none"><i>“Why not leave their private sorrows to people? Is sorrow not, one asks, the only thing in the world people really possess?”</i> <br>― Vladimir Nabokov, Pnin<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578889760i/28765935._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This isn't just the last nail in my Nabokov coffin, this is the ground thrown on the coffin. Finito sweet benito. I've now read all his ficiton (both those written in Russian and translated into English later and those written in English). It is kinda sad. But so too is Pnin. I'd call the novel melancholy, but it isn't quite sad or melancholy. There is something too sweet and funny and eccentric to be easily categorized. It is Nabokov's Don Quixote novel. His protagonist is a professor of Russian barely holding on in a fictionalized university (modeled a bit on Cornell). He isn't exactly absent minded. In fact, his mind is almost too much there. But there is something romantic and lovely about him.<br><br>The prose was beautiful and a couple chapters were near perfection. Chapter 5: Pnin drives to The Pines was amazing. I also adored the just barely intrusive narrator V.V. (Vladimir Vladimirovich). The novel wasn't my favorite Nabokov (Ada, Lolita, Pale Fire are all way better), but it is lovey and deserves a strong presence on Nabokov's slightly dusty second shelf.</span>

  <span id="freeText17144270539268247666" style="display:none"><b><br />3.70-star<br /><br />Admittedly, I found less hilarity in my second revisit due to some of his unusual lengthy sentences (an exemplary extracted one below - to add later) in which I couldn't help being hypnotized and lulled into drowsiness and soon my slumber overcame me, finding it as not being proud of such a syndrome. However, I focused on his other interesting points worth looking at and talking about to share with other Nabokov readers in this Goodreads community. <br /><br />By means of his English wordplay from some unthinkably pronounced spellings, I forgot to include these humorous extracts (not all):<br /><blockquote>'No <i>douche</i>?' inquired Pnin, looking up. '. . . I ask it, because I will not give more than a dollar per day - not including, of course, nootrition.' (p. 26)<br />. . . , and Madam Pushkin said: "You annoy me with your verses, Pushkin"- and in old age - to think only! - the wife of colossus, colossus Tolstoy liked much better than him a stoopid moozishan with a red noz!' (p. 33)<br />'It is nine hundred ninety-nine, Todd Rodd, very simple! At the very very end of the rodd, where it unites with Cleef Ahvnue. A leetle house and a beeg blahk cleef.' (p. 131)</blockquote><br />There are also some curious sentences with seemingly grammatical daring notions as follows (not all):<br /><blockquote>You also will recollect the past with interest <u>when old</u>. (p. 91)<br />Pnin <u>killed the motor</u> and sat beaming at his friends. (p. 105)<br />When everything was clean and dry, and the bowl stood aloof and serene on the safest shelf of a cupboard, and the little bright house was securely locked up in the <u>large dark night</u>, . . . (p. 152)</blockquote><br />Preferably, the reader, I think, should be trilingual, knowing Russian and German (French, anywhere?) so that he/she could read this novel with more appreciation; a reason is that the following extracts are hopelessly Greek to me.<br /><br />Yes - she never doubted that Timofey was a darling (<i>'Nu kakoy zhe ti dushka'</i>). (p. 47)<br /><br />'<i>Der arme Kerl,</i>' muttered kind-hearted Hagen to himself as he walked homeward. (p. 150)<br /><br />To continue . . .<br /><br /><b>First Review</b><br />3.75-star<br /><br />Reading "Pnin" by Vladimir Nabokov would require our familiarity regarding his writing style and his sense of humor. We may start with his "Collected Stories" (Penguin Books, 2010) since we can start with any story in which we can be interested and thus find its reading enjoyable. I would like to recommend the following:<br />1. A Letter that Never Reached Russia,<br />2. A Nursery Tale,<br />3. The Visit to the Museum<br />4. Solus Rex, and<br />5. First Love, etc.<br /><br />Linguistically, this 169-page novel has presented Pnin, an assistant professor emigrated from Russia to teach in an American campus, who daringly uses English "a language he has yet to master" (back cover) in different contexts and we can't help being amused, for instance:<br /><br />"...<br />'Information, please,' said Pnin. 'Where stops four o'clock bus to Cremona?'<br />'Right across the street,' briskly answered the employee without looking up. <br />...<br />And with the national informality that always nonplussed Pnin, the young man shoved the bag into a corner of his nook.<br />"Quittance?' queried Pnin, Englishing the Russian for 'receipt' (kvitantsiya).<br />'What's that?'<br />'Number?' tried Pnin.<br />... (p. 10)" <br /><br />And this one: "... If his Russian was music, his English was murder. He had enormous difficulty ('dzeefeecooltsee' in Pninian English) with depalatization, never managing to remove the extra Russian moisture from t's and d's before the vowels he so quaintly softened. His explosive 'hat' ('I never go in a hat even in winter') differed from the common American pronunciation of 'hot' (...) only by its briefer duration, ... (pp. 54-55)" <br /><br />Or this one, indeed, it's sheer hilarity:	"...<br />'You are very hungry?'<br />'No, sir. Not particularly.'<br />'My name is Timofey,' said Pnin, as they made themselves comfortable at a window table in the shabby old dinner. 'Second syllable pronounced as "muff", ahksent on last syllable, "ey" as in "prey" but a little more protracted. "Timofey Pavlovich Pnin", which means "Timofey the son of Paul." The pahtronymic has the ahksent on the first syllable ... (p. 89)"</b></span>
